Jamison Brereton

46 (487)
Indra
┼Ъaс╣Гyu B─Бrhaspatya
14 verses: br╠еhat─л alternating with satobr╠еhat─л, arranged in prag─Бthas
Like the previous two hymns, this one has too many verses for its position in the Indra cycle, though its division into the two-verse prag─Бtha units may account for its placement. Nonetheless, the hymn seems to be a conceptual unity, rather than a collection of independent prag─Бthas.
The hymn concentrates on Indra as the helper of his praisers in contests and in battles. He is exhorted to come to our aid, to provide us with protection, to bring

us the power inhering in rival peoples (see especially vss. 7тАУ8), and to help us defeat our foes. The hymn is without major complications, and the theme is more repeated than developed. This regular repetition gives the hymn an unhurried pace, which contrasts with the urgency of the contests and battles described. This pace begins to quicken in the last verses: the repeated phrase introducing an imperative, тАЬSo then, as everтАЭ (vss. 10, 11, 12), seems to be building to a finale. But the last prag─Бtha (vss. 13тАУ14), rather than providing the expected climactic plea to Indra for aid that would end the hymn conventionally, is instead a two-verse subordinate clause about Indra and his racing horses that utterly lacks a main clause, and it displays a den sity of imagery, with unrelated similes piled up pell-mell, that seems designed to replicate the breathless pace of a real contestтАФa remarkable effect. At the same time, it harkens back to the beginning of the hymn, with its mention of steeds and the racecourse (see vs. 1) and the тАЬgreat stakesтАЭ (see vs. 4), so that the apparently reckless speed and lack of completion of the final two verses is contained within a ring-compositional structure.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Indra This hymn nicely demonstrates the distribution of impv. forms to тИЪbh┼л that I discussed in my 1997 тАЬSyntactic constraints on morphological change: The Vedic imperatives bodhi, dehi, and dhehiтАЭ: bh├бva/bhava is found initial (3d) or final (10d, 11a) in its p─Бda or clause, while bodhi is internal (4c).

Jamison Brereton Notes

Although the presence of h├н, with its generally causal value, is often a puzzle when it appears in the first p─Бda of a hymn, this one helps signal the conceptual unity of this opening tс╣Ыca, with vs. 1 providing the various circumstances under which we call upon Indra and vs. 2 containing the contents of our latest address to the god – a call for additional generosity from him.

The poet plays with the 2nd sg. pronoun. Historically – and usually synchronically in the RV – the nom. sg. tv├бm is disyllabic (t u v├бm) and the acc. sg.

tv─Б╠Бm is monosyllabic, with occasional distraction to two syllables in analogy to the nom. But here the 1st vs. has three distracted acc. sg. t u v─Б╠Бm, prominently p─Бda-initial (a, c, d), while the nom. sg. at the beginning of the 2nd vs. is monosyllabic. To match disyllabic t u v─Б╠Бm, in 2a the two syllables are filled out by the addition of the pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ s├б (#s├б tv├бm), which is syntactically at home as subject marker of the 2nd sg. impv.

kira (see my 1992 тАЬVedic тАШs├б fig├йтАЩ: An inherited sentence connective?тАЭ). Since s├б in such contexts is unnecessary, its presence draws attention to the metrical interchange between the nom. and acc. of the pronoun here.

Griffith

THAT we may win us wealth and power we poets, verily, call on thee:
In war men call on thee, Indra, the hero’s Lord, in the steed’s race-course call on thee.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWonderful wielder of the thunderbolt, Indra, the lord of clouds, mighty in resolution, being glorified by us, grant us cattle, and horses fit for chariots, as (you grant) abundant food to him who is victorious (in battle).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The lord of clouds: adrivas = wielder of the thunderbolt; abundant food to him who is victorious: satr─Б v─Бjam na jigyuс╣гe: satr─Б = prabh┼лtam, abundant;

Jigyuс╣гe = valorous, not as applicable to a man, puruс╣г─Бya, but to a horse or elephant, a┼Ыv─Бya hastine v─Б

Jamison Brereton

So youтАФo dazzling one with mace in hand, o possessor of the stone, being praised as the great oneтАФboldly
heap up for us cow, horse, and chariotry altogether like a prize for the winner, o Indra.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Geldner takes rathy├аm as an adj. modifying ├б┼Ыvam (Wagenross); Grassmann does as well, assigning the form to the vс╣Ыk─л-inflected stem rath─л╠Б- (so also Lubotsky). Neither of these interpr. is impossible; however, I prefer to take rathy├аm as belonging to the marginal them. stem rathy├а- (beside better attested r├бthya-) and also to take it as a third term in the gifts we want from Indra. There is nothing riding on the choice of interpr., however.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Although the presence of h├н, with its generally causal value, is often a puzzle when it appears in the first p─Бda of a hymn, this one helps signal the conceptual unity of this opening tс╣Ыca, with vs. 1 providing the various circumstances under which we call upon Indra and vs. 2 containing the contents of our latest address to the god – a call for additional generosity from him.

The poet plays with the 2nd sg. pronoun. Historically – and usually synchronically in the RV – the nom. sg. tv├бm is disyllabic (t u v├бm) and the acc. sg.

tv─Б╠Бm is monosyllabic, with occasional distraction to two syllables in analogy to the nom. But here the 1st vs. has three distracted acc. sg. t u v─Б╠Бm, prominently p─Бda-initial (a, c, d), while the nom. sg. at the beginning of the 2nd vs. is monosyllabic. To match disyllabic t u v─Б╠Бm, in 2a the two syllables are filled out by the addition of the pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ s├б (#s├б tv├бm), which is syntactically at home as subject marker of the 2nd sg. impv.

kira (see my 1992 тАЬVedic тАШs├б fig├йтАЩ: An inherited sentence connective?тАЭ). Since s├б in such contexts is unnecessary, its presence draws attention to the metrical interchange between the nom. and acc. of the pronoun here.

Griffith

As such, O Wonderful, whose hand holds thunder, praised as mighty, Caster of the Stone!
Pour on us boldly, Indra, kine and chariotsteeds, ever to be the conqueror’s strength.

Geldner

Du wunderbarer Keulentr├дger Indra, sollst als der Gro├Яe besungen uns mutig Rind und Wagenro├Я zusammenbringen, wie die Beute insgesamt f├╝r den Sieger, du Herr des Pre├Яsteins.

Grassmann

So sch├╝tte du blitzhaltender, o gl├дnzender, du hochgepriesner Schleuderer, Uns k├╝hnlich zu, o Indra, Rind und Wagenpferd zusammt als Preis dem Siegenden.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWe invoke that Indra who is the desroyer of mighty foes, the supervisor (of all things); do you, the many organed, the protector of the good, the distributor of wealth, be unto us (the insurer of) success in combats.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The unbounded one who smites altogether, that Indra do we call upon. O you possessing a thousand testicles, of powerful manliness, master of settlementsтАФbe there to strengthen us in the combats.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. shows some continuities with the previous prag─Бtha: the root noun cmpd satr─Бh─Б╠Б(3a) echoes satr─Б╠Бin 2d, as satpate (3c) does s├бtpatim in 1c. There is also the variant 1st pl. mid. root form to тИЪh┼л/hv─Б, h┼лmahe (3b), which contrasts with h├бv─Бmahe in 1a. I can see no difference in sense here, and I think there are several other factors at work. On the one hand, extremely common h├бv─Бmahe (+/- accent) is almost never p─Бda-internal, whereas the rare-ish h┼лm├бhe (+/- accent) appears about half the time in that position (but see p─Бda-final h┼лmahe in 6b) – so it partly replicates the bh├бva/bodhi distribution discussed above. But perhaps more important is that the poet seems to be playing with metrical variants in a way similar to s├б tv├бm discussed above. P─Бda b reads ├нndraс╣Г t├бс╣Г h┼лmahe vay├бm. There is no good reason for t├бm because ├нndram more than sufficiently provides the acc. obj.; moreover, all things being equal, t├бm (and its paradigmatic fellows) generally opens its p─Бda/clause and in particular does not follow a coreferential noun. The common 1st pl. hav─Бmahe, already found in 1a, would easily fit in a version of this p─Бda that lacked the t├бm:

*├нndraс╣Г hav─Бmahe vay├бm. I suggest that the poet called attention to his manipulation of the variant verb forms by inserting a pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ t├бm, like the pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ s├б in 2a, and inserting it in the тАЬwrongтАЭ place, which would draw the attention of his audience even more.

We call upon that Indra, who, most active, ever slays the foe:
Lord of the brave, Most Manly, with a thousand powers, help thou and prosper us in fight.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬSuch, Indra, as you are represented in holy texts, assail (our) adversaries with fierceness like (that of) a bull in close conflict; regard yourself as our defender in war, (that we may long enjoy) posterity, water, and the sight of the) sun.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Represented in holy texts: с╣Ыc─лс╣гama = с╣Ыgy─Бdс╣Ы┼Ыam r┼лpam pratip─Бdayati t─Бdс╣Ыg r┼лpendraс╕е, such form as the с╣Ъc exhibits, such in form is Indra; posterity, water and sun: tanuс╣гu, apsu, s┼лrye = in descendants, in waters, in the sun; connecting this what precedes: asm─Бkam bodhi avit─Б mah─Бdhane, know yourself to be our protector in battle, in descendants, etc., that is in securing to us these good things and long life

Jamison Brereton

Like a bull, you press the peoples hard with your battle fury at the spirited competition, o you who are equal to song.
Become our helper at the (contest for) great stakes, at (the contest for) our bodies, the waters, and the sun.

Jamison Brereton Notes

vс╣Ыс╣гabh├йva is somewhat problematic: the Pp. analyzes it as vс╣Ыс╣гabh─Б╠Бiva, which is phonologically impeccable, but what form would vс╣Ыс╣гabh─Б╠Бrepresent? OldenbergтАЩs solution (flg. Lanman, Noun Inflection 329) that it is an underlying nom. sg. is surely the most likely, whether we subscribe to LanmanтАЩs тАЬcrasis after elision [of the s],тАЭ i.e., vс╣Ыс╣гabh├бs iva ├а vс╣Ыс╣гabha iva ├а -e-. The published translation, as well as Geldner, implicitly follows this route. A long-shot possibility is that the Pp. vс╣Ыс╣гabh─Б╠Бis the underlying form, and itтАЩs an old instr. sg. modifying or doubling many├║n─Б: тАЬwith bullish battlefuryтАЭ or тАЬwith battle-fury as a bull.тАЭ But vс╣Ы╠Бс╣гan- is the тАШbullтАЩ stem generally used (quasi-)adjectivally, not vс╣Ыс╣гabh├б-. It might also be possible to see it as a voc. vс╣Ыс╣гabha: this would easily account for the sandhi, but we would have to assume it got secondarily accented after it was no longer understood as a voc., and this would also introduce the interpretational problem of a voc. in a simile (though unfortunately there are a few such).

On the problematic с╣Ыc─лс╣гama see the despairing comm. ad I.61.1.

The three loc. in d specify the тАШstakesтАЩ (dhan├б-) referred to by mah─Бdhan├й.

Griffith

Rcisama, thou forcest men as with a bull, with anger, in the furious fray.
Be thou our Helper in the mighty battle fought for sunlight, water, and for life.

Geldner

Du bedr├дngst die V├╢lker wie ein Bulle im Zorn, in dem erregten Kampf, du ……. Sei uns Sch├╝tzer im gro├Яen Kampf um die eigenen Leiber, um Wasser und Sonne!

Grassmann

Du st├╢sst im Zorn hinweg die Menschen wie ein Stier, in wilder Schlacht, o gl├дnzender; Sei unser Helfer du im grossen Kampfgew├╝hl um Leiber, Wasser, Sonnenlicht.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬIndra, bring to us most excellent, most invigorating and nutritious food, wherewith, wonderful wielder of the thunderbolt, the handsome-chinned, you sustain both heaven and earth.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Indra, bring us the best, the mightiest, fulfilling fame,
with which you, o dazzling one with mace in hand, fill both these
world-halves, o you of good lips.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The voc. phrase in c, citra vajrahasta, is repeated from 2a.

The verb pr─Б╠Бс╕е must be read disyllabically. It could therefore technically be a subjunctive (so apparently Grassmann), and in fact the light first syllable required could reflect the loss of root-final laryngeal before the subjunctive suffixal vowel.

Hoffmann insistently calls it an injunc. (215 n. 201, 221), fld. by Lubotsky, and the published

tr. (тАЬyou тАж fillтАЭ) reflects an injunctive interpr. But since this is not a cosmogonic act

– Indra is filling the world halves with тАЬfameтАЭ – a subjunctive interpr. is possible, esp. following the impv.: тАЬbring us fame with which you will fill both these worldhalves.тАЭ I consider this a possible, perhaps even desirable alternative.

Griffith

O Indra, bring us name and fame, enriching, mightiest, excellent,
Wherewith, O Wondrous God, fair-visored, thunder-armed, thou hast filled full this earth and heaven.

Geldner

Grassmann

Bring, Indra, besten, kr├дftigsten und reichgef├╝llten Schatz uns her, Mit dem du beide Welten f├╝llst, o gl├дnzender, Blitztr├дger, mit dem Helm geziert.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWe invoke for protection you, royal Indra, who are mighty among the gods, tthe subduer of men; granter of dwellings, repel all evil spirits, and render our enemies easy of discomfiture.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

It is you, the mighty conqueror of the bordered domains, that we call upon to help, o king among the gods.
Make all our things that waver gain foothold and our foes be easily
conquered, o good one.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The distracted acc. t u v─Б╠Бm from 1a, c, d returns here, again as object of тАЬwe call,тАЭ but with h┼лmahe rather than the h├бv─Бmahe of vs. 1.

On pibdan├б- see also comm. ad IX.15.6. The stem is obviously derived from a redupl. form of тИЪpad; cf. the hapax med. part. p├нbdam─Бna- (X.101.11). The orig.

sense is likely тАШkeep stepping, go step-by-step,тАЩ vel sim., as an iter. See EWA s.v. PAD, with ref. to Strunk and Got┼Н. A literal gloss could then be тАШplod, trudgeтАЩ. This literal sense is seen, in my view, in the participle in X.101.11 v├бhnir ─Бp├нbdam─Бnaс╕е тАШplodding draught horseтАЩ, and in IX.15.6 I also take v├бs┼лni pibdan─Б╠Бas a somewhat comic reference to cows as тАЬplodding goods.тАЭ Our passage here is more difficult, as the word is contrasted with vithur├б- тАШwaveringтАЩ; this opposition accounts for the standard glosses as (Grassmann) тАШfeststehend, festтАЩ, (AiG II.2.595 тАШfestтАЩ (but .203 тАШerstarkendтАЩ), (Geldner) тАШdauerhaftтАЩ, (Renou) тАШsolidesтАЩ (both latter in IX.15.6), but my тАШgain(ing) a footholdтАЩ seems to preserve the root etymology and sense, while fitting the context.

Griffith

We call on thee, O King, Mighty amid the Gods, Ruler of men, to succour us.
All that is weak in us, Excellent God, make firm: make our foes easy to subdue.

Geldner

Dich, den gewaltigen V├╢lkerbezwinger, du K├╢nig ├╝ber die G├╢tter, rufen wir zum Schutze an. Mache fein alles Unsichere bei uns fest auftretend, du Guter, mache die Feinde leicht zu bezwingen!

Grassmann

Dich rufen wir, den starken Menschenb├дndiger, o G├╢tterf├╝rst, zu H├╝lfe dich; Mach recht, o guter, fest uns alles wankende, die Feinde leicht besiegbar uns.

English translation:

тАЬWhatever strength and opulence (exist) among human beings, whatever be the sustenance of the five classes of men, bring Indra to us, as well (as) all great manly energies.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The might and manliness that are in the Nahuс╣гa territories, Indra, or the brilliance that belongs to the five settlementsтАФbring them here: all the masculine powers altogether.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The main cl. begins in the middle (or rather towards the end) of p─Бda c, with ─Б╠Бbhara. Since this phrase is only 3 syllables, the audience would not mistake the syntactic break for a p─Бda break despite the extra length of the c-p─Бda in Bс╣Ыhat─л.

satr─Б╠Бreappears once again (cf. 2d, 3a).

Or what bullish power is in Tr╠еkс╣гi and in the Druhyu people, or whatever is in the P┼лru, o bounteous one,
grant that to us fully at (the time of) the conquering of men, for us to vanquish our foes in battles.

English translation:

тАЬAffluent Indra, propitiated by praise, be nigh o us, as the defender of our person ns (against those) who assail (us as) enemies, with a mind bent upon carrying off (our) cattle, or who assault us with arrogance.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Who, with their mind set on cattle, outwit their rival and smite him boldly.
So then, as ever, o bounteous Indra longing for songs, as the protector of our bodies, come up close to us.

Griffith

They who with minds intent on spoil subdue the foe, boldly attack and smite him down,-
From these, O Indra Maghavan who lovest song, be closest guardian of our lives.

Jamison Brereton

So then, as ever, be there to strengthen us. Indra, help our leader in the fight,
when the feathered, sharp-headed arrows fly in the midspace,

Jamison Brereton Notes

The first p─Бda is a bare variant of 3d: both contain the complex verbal construction vс╣Ыdh├й тИЪbh┼л тАШbe for strengthening / be there to strengthenтАЩ, each with the complement enclitic naс╕е. The only difference is the placement of the impv.: initial bh├бv─Б in 3d, final bhava in 11a, differing only in accent and, possibly, in the length of the final vowel: the Pp. resolves the cross-p─Бda sandhi bhav├йndra with short bhava, like 10d, but a long vowel would be equally possible.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This prag─Бtha shows both internal and external connections. As in the previous prag─Бtha the 2nd vs. is syntactically dependent on the first, with the y├бtra clause of 12ab parallel to the y├бd clause of 11cd and both subordinate to the imperatival clause of 11b. In addition the first and last hemistichs (11ab, 12cd) open identically, with ├бdha sm─Б, which echoes ├бdha sm─Б of the last hemistich of the previous prag─Бtha (10cd).

On the particular connection of vs. 12 with vs. 9 see immed. below.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWhen heroes rest their person ns (until abandoning) the plural asant abodes of their progenitors; grant us, for ourselves and our posterity, an unsuspected defence, and scatter our enemies.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The plural asant abode of their progenitors: priy─Б ┼Ыarma pitс╣Ыс╣З─Бm = priy─Бс╣Зi sth─Бn─Бni janak─Бn─Бm sambandhin─л, the beloved plural ces in relation with progenitors; tanvo vitanvate, they spread out or rest their person ns; or, they spread out before the enemy the sites won by their forefathers until they abandon them, parityajanti y─Бvat; i.e. until they, the enemy, desis fom the attack; an unsuspected defence: chardiracittam = kavacam ┼Ыatrubhir aj├▒─Бtam, armour unknown by the enemies

Jamison Brereton

Where champions stretch wide their own dear bodies as shelter for their ancestors.
So then, as ever, hold out protection to our body and lineage. Keep
away unforeseen hatred.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The published translation contains a grammatical error. It takes priy─Б╠Бas modifying fem.

pl. tanv├ас╕е, but the sandhi context of priy─Б╠Бmakes this impossible: it would have to be

*priy─Б╠Бс╕е. It must modify ┼Ы├бrma (as Geldner takes it, also Grassmann), which must then be a neut.

plural to the -an-stem. This tr. should be corrected to тАЬтАж stretch wide their own bodies as dear shelters тАжтАЭ Geldner takes tanv├ас╕е and ┼Ы├бrma as implicitly conjoined (тАЬWo die Tapferen ihre Leiber breit machen (and) die lieben Schilde des VatersтАЭ), but I think it more likely that the champions are stretching their bodes to serve as shelters/shields. Under this interpr. the plural of ┼Ы├бrma makes sense: multiple bodies multiple shields.

P─Бdas abc strongly echo 9abc, with the c p─Бdas esp. close, both containing the VP chard├нr yacha (in opposite order and non-contiguous in 12c) + conjoined DAT. beneficiary (9c magh├бvadbhya┼Ы ca m├бhyaс╣Г ca; 12c tanv├и t├бne ca). However, the first hemistich of 12 varies tellingly from 9ab: in 9 it is Indra who holds out the shelter (┼Ыaraс╣З├бm), which is equated with the chard├нс╕е of c, but in 12 it is the mortal champions (┼Ы┼л╠Бr─Бsaс╕е) who offer their own bodies as shelter (┼Ы├бrma). (Although the two words for тАШshelterтАЩ are different, they are transparently related and share the same descriptors elsewhere.) The bodies of the mortal warriors are theirs to deploy, but also under the protection of Indra, as shown not only by tanv├и in the next p─Бda as recipient of IndraтАЩs protection, but also by tan┼лp─Б╠Бс╕е тАШprotector of bodiesтАЩ in 10d, applied to Indra.

The last p─Бdas of vss. 9 and 12 provide the final thread of connection between the two vss., since both contain the impv. y─Бv├бy─Б╠Ж тАШkeep awayтАЩ. In 12d the accent on the verb is anomalous, but I have no trouble assuming that it was adapted from 9d, where the initial position of the verb requires it.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This prag─Бtha shows both internal and external connections. As in the previous prag─Бtha the 2nd vs. is syntactically dependent on the first, with the y├бtra clause of 12ab parallel to the y├бd clause of 11cd and both subordinate to the imperatival clause of 11b. In addition the first and last hemistichs (11ab, 12cd) open identically, with ├бdha sm─Б, which echoes ├бdha sm─Б of the last hemistich of the previous prag─Бtha (10cd).

On the particular connection of vs. 12 with vs. 9 see immed. below.

Griffith

Give us, where heroes strain their bodies in the fight, the shelter that our fathers loved.
To us and to our sons give refuge: keep afar all unobserved hostility.

Wo die Tapferen ihre Leiber breit machen und die lieben Schilde der V├дter, da gew├дhre uns selbst und den Kindern Schutz! Wehre die unerwartete Feindschaft ab!

Wo ihre Leiber Helden dehnen breit und hoch, der V├дter lieben Schutz und Schirm, Da reiche du uns und den Kindern deinen Schutz, treib unversehns die Feinde fort.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(At the time) when, in the effort (made) in an arduous conflict, you urge our horses over an uneven road, like falcons darting upon their food through the difficult path (of the firmament).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

When, Indra, at the charge, you will spur on your steeds at the (contest for) great stakes,
on an uncrowded (race)course, on its twisting path, like falcons
hunting fame,

Jamison Brereton Notes

The form ├бrvataс╕е ends the first p─Бda of this vs. and the last of vs. 1, but with different grammatical identities: gen. sg. in 1d, acc. pl. here. This difference may be one indication that this prag─Бtha both responds to the rest of the hymn and distances itself from it.

mah─Бdhan├й is repeated from 4c.

13-14 ...{Loading}...
Jamison Brereton Notes

As was noted in the published introduction, this last prag─Бtha stands somewhat apart from the rest of the hymn, though it does show connections with the beginning of the hymn. The near repetition found between the last two prag─Бthas (9-10 / 11-12; see disc. above) gave the sense that the hymn was coming to an end. As often, RVic poets seem to enjoy shaking up our structural expectations. The lack of a main clause in the whole of this two-vs. complex is especially striking and ends the hymn on an unsettled and somewhat frenzied note.

Griffith

When, Indra, in the mighty fray thou urgest chargers to their speed,
On the uneven road and on a toilsome path, like falcons, eager for renown,

Geldner

Wenn du, Indra, die Rennpferde im Rennen um den gro├Яen Preis antreiben wirst auf der Stra├Яe, wo es keine Zusammenst├╢├Яe gibt, auf gekr├╝mmtem Wege, die schnell wie die Falken nach dem Ruhmespreis streben,

Grassmann

Wenn Indra mit des Renners Flug die gier’gen Rosse Adlern gleich Auf ungebahntem Wege auf gekr├╝mmtem Pfad, du antreibst in der grossen Schlacht,

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬRushing rapidly like rivers in their downward course and although neighing loudly through trro, they yet, tight-girthed, return repeatedly (to the conflict) for cattle, like birds darting on their prey.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

(The steeds) going swiftly like rivers in a torrent when (the chariot?) has sounded following their roar,
who, like birds over raw flesh, keep circling (the racecourse), being held firm in the cow [=leather (reins)] in your two arms…

Jamison Brereton Notes

While vs. 13 has a relatively perspicuous structure – a single transitive y├бd clause whose final p─Бda is a simile matching the acc. direct object – vs. 14 is a structural mess. Its first p─Бda is another simile in the acc. matching the direct object of vs. 13; it is followed by a y├бdi (or *y├бd ─л; see below) clause (b), which may or may not contain a verb, followed by a rel. cl. (cd) introduced by y├й, containing another simile referring to the same direct object but now in the nom. By now the original referent is quite distant, and it is not entirely clear which parts belong to the simile, which to the frame.

P─Бda b is esp. problematic, mostly because of the ambiguity of the phrase ├бnu с╣гv├бс╣Зi, in which sv├бni has been identified variously as a noun or as a verb. The preponderance of opinion favors the former: Whitney (┬з390b, though see Roots, where he lists it, with ?, as an aor.), Grassmann (though he allows for the other poss.), Geldner, Lubotsky On the other hand, Oldenberg, flg. BR, considers it a verb form, a passive aorist.

Wackernagel (AiG III.23) is uncertain. The noun-faction is further divided by what stem they assign it to: neut. -i-stem (Grassmann, Lubotsky), root noun in -an- (Wh, and presumably Geldner, since he tr. it as a loc. тАЬim Get├╢seтАЭ). If it is a noun (тАШsoundтАЩ, vel sim.), a verb needs to be supplied with ├бnu, but this of course would pose no problem. I am always reluctant to oppose Oldenberg, and in this particular case there is strong objective evidence that he is correct, namely the close sandhi effect that retroflexes с╣г after ├бnu. A collection of all s-forms after ├бnu produces remarkably clear-cut results: ├бnu only retroflexes following verb forms, never nominal forms.

Although it may seem overkill to list all the examples, the collection may be useful for other purposes: ├бnu + VERB: I.167.10 [=182.8, III.39.8] ├бnu с╣гy─Бt, I.185.4 ├бnu с╣гy─Бma, V.73.4 ├бnu с╣гс╣н├бve, VIII.3.8 ├бnu с╣гс╣нuvanti. There is only one verbal form without retroflexion: IV.4.2 ├бnu spr┼Ы╠е a.

├бnu + NOMINAL (etc.): I.33.11 [=I.88.6, 176.2, III.51.11, IV.33.6, 52.6, VII.56.13, VIII.88.5] ├бnu svadh─Б╠Бm, I.80.1 (etc.) ├бnu svar─Б╠Бjyam, I.121.3 ├бnu svaj─Б╠Бm, I.134.1 ├бnu s┼лnс╣Ы╠Бt─Б, I.191.15 ├бnu saс╣Гv├бtaс╕е, III.7.6 [=V.59.1, IX.63.6] ├бnu sv├бm, III.33.3 ├бnu saс╣Гc├бrant─л, III.35.8 ├бnu sv─Б╠Бс╕е, IV.40.4 ├бnu saс╣Гt├бv─лtvat, IV.45.6 ├бnu svadh├бy─Б, V.32.10 ├бnu svadh─Б╠Бvne, V.34.1 ├бnu svadh─Бmit─Б, VI.25.8 ├бnu s├бho, VII.7.2 [X.14.2] ├бnu sv─Б╠Бс╕е, VII.31.7 ├бnu svadh─Б╠Бvar─л, VIII.4.8 ├бnu spighy├аm, VIII.6.38 ├бnu suv─Бn─Б╠Бsa, IX.103.5 ├бnu svadh─Б╠Бс╕е, X.17.11 ├бnu saс╣Гc├бrantam, X.17.11 ├бnu sapt├б, X.56.3 ├бnu saty─Б╠Б, X.103.6 ├бnu s├бm.

Among the nominals it is striking how many begin with sv- as in our case.

I can see only one possible conclusion, that Oldenberg must be right, this is a 3rd sg.

passive (or rather, intransitive) aor., and we need to supply a subject. Oldenberg suggests chariot, which seems reasonable. As he points out, the RV has a bahuvr─лhi svan├бdratha- lit. тАШhaving a sounding chariotтАЩ (though prob. used as a PN), and a chariot sounding тАЬfollowing the roar (of the horses)тАЭ makes sense. II.4.6 v─Б╠Бr с╣З├б path─Б╠Б r├бthyeva sv─Бn─лt тАЬlike water along a path, like chariot (wheels) he has soundedтАЭ provides a parallel not only for the sounding chariot, but also for the rushing, sounding rivers in the simile of p─Бda a.

A few other loose ends in p─Бda b: I interpr. y├бdi as *y├бd ─л тАЬwhen it,тАЭ with shortening of ─л before the cluster kl. A condition тАШifтАЩ doesnтАЩt make sense. As for that cluster, kl├│┼Ыa- is the only -l-form to тИЪkru┼Ы тАШcry outтАЩ. Is this racetrack slang? P─Бda c compares the steeds circling the race course to birds (of prey) circling over the raw flesh of a dead animal (a striking image). Geldner considers the loc. g├бvi to be the correspondent of ─Б╠Бmiс╣гi in the simile: the cow is the prize over which the horses circle (тАЬdie wie die V├╢gel um das Aas, so um die Kuh(herde) kreisenтАЭ). The published translation by contrast takes g├бvi as a piece of horse tack, the reins or something else made of leather, and construes it with gс╣Ыbh─лt─Б╠Бс╕е. I now favor GeldnerтАЩs interpr., which is more striking and which also conforms to the loc. of the stake found several times in this hymn. I would amend the tr. to тАЬwho, like birds over raw flesh, keep circling (the race course) over the bovine (prize), being held firm in your two arms тАжтАЭ

Jamison Brereton Notes

As was noted in the published introduction, this last prag─Бtha stands somewhat apart from the rest of the hymn, though it does show connections with the beginning of the hymn. The near repetition found between the last two prag─Бthas (9-10 / 11-12; see disc. above) gave the sense that the hymn was coming to an end. As often, RVic poets seem to enjoy shaking up our structural expectations. The lack of a main clause in the whole of this two-vs. complex is especially striking and ends the hymn on an unsettled and somewhat frenzied note.

Griffith

Speeding like rivers rushing down a steep descent, responsive to the urging call,
That come like birds attracted to the bait, held in by reins in both the driver’s hands.

Geldner

Die rasch wie die Fl├╝sse im Gef├дlle gehen, wann sie im Get├╢se dem Zuruf folgen, die wie die V├╢gel um das Aas, so um die Kuhherde kreisen, in beiden Armen mit dem Z├╝gel festgehalten.

Grassmann

Wie Str├╢me, die am Abhang gehn in schnellem Schuss, wenn’s rauschet deinem Zuruf nach, Sie, die wie V├╢gel auf das Fleisch sich st├╝rzen hin, gefasst am Z├╝gel mit dem Arm.

Elizarenkova

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдЗрд╕ рдордиреНрддреНрд░ рдореЗрдВ рдЙрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдЩреНрдХрд╛рд░ рд╣реИред рд╣реЗ рдордиреБрд╖реНрдпреЛ ! рддреБрдо рдЬреИрд╕реЗ рдЬрд▓ рдКрдБрдЪреЗ рд╕реНрдерд╛рди рд╕реЗ рдиреАрдЪреЗ рдХреЗ рд╕реНрдерд╛рди рдХреЛ рд╢реАрдШреНрд░ рдЬрд╛рддрд╛ рд╣реИ рдФрд░ рдЬреИрд╕реЗ рдмрд╛рдЬ рдЖрджрд┐ рдкрдХреНрд╖реА рдорд╛рдБрд╕ рдХреЗ рд▓рд┐рдпреЗ рд╢реАрдШреНрд░ рдЬрд╛рддреЗ рд╣реИрдВ, рд╡реИрд╕реЗ рднреВрдорд┐, рдЕрдиреНрддрд░рд┐рдХреНрд╖ рд╡рд╛ рдЬрд▓ рдореЗрдВ рд╡рд╛рд╣рдиреЛрдВ рд╕реЗ рд╢реАрдШреНрд░ рдЬрд╛рдУ реерезрекрее рдЗрд╕ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдореЗрдВ рд░рд╛рдЬрд╛, рд╡реАрд░, рд╕рдЩреНрдЧреНрд░рд╛рдо, рдЧреГрд╣, рд╢реВрд░рд╡реАрд░ рдФрд░ рдпрд╛рди рдХреГрддреНрдп рдХреЗ рд╡рд░реНрдгрди рд╕реЗ рдЗрд╕ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдХреА рдкреВрд░реНрд╡ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрде рдХреЗ рд╕рд╛рде рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐ рдЬрд╛рдирдиреА рдЪрд╛рд╣рд┐рдпреЗ рее рдпрд╣ рдЫрдпрд╛рд▓реАрд╕рд╡рд╛рдБ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдФрд░ рдЙрдирддреАрд╕рд╡рд╛рдБ рд╡рд░реНрдЧ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрдд рд╣реБрдЖ рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдпрдГ

рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдп: рд╣реЗ рд░рд╛рдЬрдиреН ! рднрд╡рд╛рдиреН рдпрджрд┐ рдкреНрд░рд╡рдгреЗ рд╕рд┐рдиреНрдзреВрдирд┐рд╡рд╛рд╢реБрдпрд╛ рд╕реНрд╡рдиреНрдпрд╛рдорд┐рд╖рд┐ рд╡рдпреЛ рди рдЧрд╡рд┐ рдХреНрд▓реЛрд╢рдордиреБрд╡рд░реНрд╡реГрддрддрд┐ред рдмрд╛рд╣реНрд╡реЛрд░реНрдЧреГрднреАрддрд╛ рд░рд╢реНрдордпрдГ рдХрд▓рд╛ рд╡рд╛ рдпрдерд╛рд╡рдЪреНрдЪрд▓рдиреНрддрд┐ рддрд░реНрд╣рд┐ рд╕реНрдерд╛рдирд╛рдиреНрддрд░рдкреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрддрд┐рд░реНрджреБрд░реНрд▓рднрд╛ рдирд╛рд╕реНрддрд┐ рдпреЗ рдпрддреЛ рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрдиреНрддреНрдпрд╛рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрдиреНрддрд┐ рддреЗрд╜рдкреНрдпреЗрд╡рдордиреБрддрд┐рд╖реНрдардиреНрддреБ реерезрекрее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрдГ

рдкреБрдирд╕реНрддреЗ рд░рд╛рдЬрд╛рджрдпрдГ рдХрд┐рдВ рдХреБрд░реНрдпреНрдпреБрд░рд┐рддреНрдпрд╛рд╣ рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рд╕рд┐рдиреНрдзреВрдирд┐рд╡) рдирджреАрд░рд┐рд╡ (рдкреНрд░рд╡рдгреЗ) рдирд┐рдореНрдирд╕реНрдерд╛рдиреЗ (рдЖрд╢реБрдпрд╛) рдЖрд╢реБрдЧреИрд░рд╢реНрд╡реИрдГ (рдпрддрдГ) рдпрд╕реНрдорд╛рддреН (рдпрджрд┐) (рдХреНрд▓реЛрд╢рдореН) рдХреНрд░реЛрд╢рдореН (рдЕрдиреБ) (рд╕реНрд╡рдирд┐) рд╢рдмреНрджреЗ (рдЖ) (рдпреЗ) (рд╡рдпрдГ) рдкрдХреНрд╖рд┐рдгрдГ (рди) рдЗрд╡ (рд╡рд░реНрд╡реГрддрддрд┐) рднреГрд╢рдВ рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрддрд┐ (рдЖрдорд┐рд╖рд┐) рдорд╛рдВрд╕реЗ рджреГрд╖реНрдЯреЗ рд╕рддрд┐ (рдЧреГрднреАрддрд╛рдГ) рдЧреГрд╣реАрддрд╛рдГ (рдмрд╛рд╣реНрд╡реЛрдГ) (рдЧрд╡рд┐) рдкреГрдерд┐рд╡реНрдпрд╛рдореН реерезрекрее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдЕрддреНрд░реЛрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдЩреНрдХрд╛рд░рдГред рд╣реЗ рдордиреБрд╖реНрдпрд╛ ! рдпреВрдпрдВ рдпрдереЛрджрдХрдореБрдЪреНрдЪрд╕реНрдерд╛рдирд╛рдиреН рдирд┐рдореНрдирдВ рджреЗрд╢рдВ рд╕рджреНрдпреЛ рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрддрд┐ рдпрдерд╛ рд╡рд╛ рд╢реНрдпреЗрдирд╛рджрдпрдГ рдкрдХреНрд╖рд┐рдгреЛ рдорд╛рдВрд╕рд╛рд░реНрдердВ рддреВрд░реНрдгрдВ рдзрд╛рд╡рдиреНрддрд┐ рддрдереИрд╡ рднреВрдореНрдпрдиреНрддрд░рд┐рдХреНрд╖реЗ рдЬрд▓реЗ рд╡рд╛ рдпрд╛рдиреИрдГ рд╕рджреНрдпреЛ рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрддреЗрддрд┐ реерезрекрее рдЕрддреНрд░ рд░рд╛рдЬрд╡реАрд░рд╕рдЩреНрдЧреНрд░рд╛рдордЧреГрд╣рд╢реВрд░рд╡реАрд░рдпрд╛рдирдХреГрддреНрдпрд╡рд░реНрдгрдирд╛рджреЗрддрджрд░реНрдерд╕реНрдп рдкреВрд░реНрд╡рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрдереЗрди рд╕рд╣ рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐рд░реНрд╡реЗрджреНрдпрд╛ рее рдЗрддрд┐ рд╖рдЯреНрдЪрддреНрд╡рд╛рд░рд┐рдВрд╢рддреНрддрдордВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдореЗрдХреЛрдирддреНрд░рд┐рдВрд╢реЛ рд╡рд░реНрдЧрд╢реНрдЪ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГ рее

рд╕рд╡рд┐рддрд╛ рдЬреЛрд╢реА тЖР рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рдо) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдпрд╛ рдордВрддреНрд░рд╛рдд рдЙрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдВрдХрд╛рд░ рдЖрд╣реЗ. рд╣реЗ рдорд╛рдгрд╕рд╛рдВрдиреЛ ! рдЬрд▓ рдЬрд╕реЗ рдЙрдВрдЪ рд╕реНрдерд╛рдирд╛рд╡рд░реВрди рдЦрд╛рд▓рдЪреНрдпрд╛ рд╕реНрдерд╛рдиреА рддрд╛рддреНрдХрд╛рд│ рдкреЛрд╣реЛрдЪрддреЗ рд╡ рдЬрд╕реЗ рд╕рд╕рд╛рдгрд╛ рдЗрддреНрдпрд╛рджреА рдкрдХреНрд╖реА рдорд╛рдВрд╕рд╛рд╕рд╛рдареА рддрддреНрдХрд╛рд│ рдЬрд╛рддрд╛рдд рддрд╕реЗ рддреБрдореНрд╣реА рд╡рд╛рд╣рдирд╛рдВрджреНрд╡рд╛рд░реЗ рднреВрдореА, рдЕрдВрддрд░рд┐рдХреНрд╖ рдХрд┐рдВрд╡рд╛ рдЬрд▓рд╛рдд рддрд╛рддреНрдХрд╛рд│ рдЬрд╛. рее резрек рее
